Thus any non-sunny line that avoids the two triples covers at most 2 points. So if we use a non-sunny line covering only 2 points, then we have 13 points to cover with 4 sunny lines, max 12 distinct points, so impossible. So k=4 is impossible for n=5.

What about k=0? Already possible. k=1? We gave example. k=2? Attempted and seems impossible. k=3? We gave example. k=4? Impossible. k=5? Impossible. So for n=5, possible k are 0,1,3. That's interesting: not all values.

Now for n=6? Let's try to see pattern. Points: a,b≥1, a+b≤7. Number of points: sum_{i=1}^{6} i =21. We have 6 lines. Need to determine possible k.

We need to analyze in general. This problem is from an Olympiad (maybe IMO Shortlist?). The answer likely: k can be any integer from 0 to n such that k ≠ 2? Or maybe k can be 0,1,3,4,5,...? Let's test n=5 gave 0,1,3. For n=6, we need to see if k=2 possible, k=4 possible, etc.

We need a general approach.

Observation: The points are all integer lattice points in the triangle T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a+b ≤ n+1}. We have n lines. The coverage condition: each point of T_n lies on at least one of the n lines.

Define the set of lines. Among the lines, some are sunny (slope not 0,∞,-1). The three families of non-sunny lines: horizontal, vertical, slope -1.

We need to determine possible number of sunny lines.

Idea: Use combinatorial incidence counting or double counting. Consider the number of points on each line. Since we have n lines, and total points = n(n+1)/2, we have sum over lines of (number of points on that line) ≥ n(n+1)/2. But also each line can contain at most some number of points from the triangle.

For a given line, the maximum number of points from T_n it can contain depends on its slope and intercept. For a line that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, what is the maximum number of points from T_n it can contain? This is essentially the maximum number of integer lattice points on a line that lie within the triangle. For a line with slope m, the points (a, ma+c) integer. The triangle condition: a ≥ 1, b = ma+c ≥ 1, a + b ≤ n+1 => a + ma + c ≤ n+1 => a(1+m) ≤ n+1-c. So the number of integer a ≥ 1 satisfying a ≤ (n+1-c)/(1+m) and also b≥1 => a ≥ (1-c)/m if m>0. For m positive, the number of a is roughly floor((n+1-c)/(1+m)) - max(1, ceil((1-c)/m)) + 1. This can be large if m is small or negative. But for sunny lines, we cannot have m=0,∞,-1. So we could have lines with slope 1 (which is sunny) that can contain up to floor((n+1)/2) points? For y=x, the points are (a,a) with a+b=2a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So number of points = floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, floor(6/2)=3. For n=6, floor(7/2)=3? Actually (n+1)/2 = 3.5, floor=3. So y=x gives 3 points. For y=x+1, points (a,a+1) with sum=2a+1 ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor(n/2). For n=6, floor(6/2)=3, but check a=3 gives b=4 sum7= n+1, ok; so 3 points. So slope1 lines can have up to floor((n+1)/2) points, which is about n/2. For n=6, max 3. For n=7, max 4. So slope1 lines can cover many points.

Similarly, slope 2 lines: y=2x+c gives sum=3x+c ≤ n+1 => x ≤ floor((n+1-c)/3). For c=0, max floor((n+1)/3). For n=6, floor(7/3)=2. So max 2 points. So slope1 lines are the best among sunny lines.

Slope -2 lines: y=-2x+c gives sum = -x+c ≤ n+1 => x ≥ c - (n+1). Also b = -2x+c ≥ 1 => x ≤ (c-1)/2. So the number of integer x with x ≥1 and x ≤ floor((c-1)/2) and x ≥ c-(n+1). This can be large if c is chosen appropriately. For n=6, we can get up to? For c=7: points (1,5) sum6, (2,3) sum5, (3,1) sum4 => 3 points. That's a triple. For c=8: (1,6) sum7, (2,4) sum6, (3,2) sum5, (4,0) no; so 3 points? Actually (1,6) sum7, (2,4) sum6, (3,2) sum5; that's 3 points. For c=9: (1,7) sum8>7? n+1=7, so (1,7) sum8>7 not in triangle; (2,5) sum7, (3,3) sum6, (4,1) sum5; that's 3 points: (2,5),(3,3),(4,1). So slope -2 lines can also have 3 points for n=6. So max sunny lines can have 3 points for n=6. Could they have 4? Possibly if m=1/2? But then a must be even, so number of points limited by triangle. For n=6, line y=(1/2)x+c: points with a even: a=2,4,6. For c=0: (2,1) sum3, (4,2) sum6, (6,3) sum9>7 no; so 2 points. For c=1: (2,2) sum4, (4,3) sum7, (6,4) sum10>7; so 2 points. So max 2. So slope1 lines are best.

Thus the maximum number of points a sunny line can contain from T_n is floor((n+1)/2) for slope1 lines? Actually for n=6, floor(7/2)=3. For n=7, floor(8/2)=4. For n=5, floor(6/2)=3. So the maximum is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Similarly, there are other slopes that may also achieve that number? For n=6, slope1 lines can have 3 points. Could a slope -2 line have 4? For n=6, slope -2: we found 3 points. For slope -1/2? Probably 2. So the maximum is floor((n+1)/2). For n=6, that's 3. For n=7, that's 4. So the average points per line if we use all sunny lines is (n(n+1)/2)/n = (n+1)/2, which is exactly the maximum. So to cover all points with n lines, we would need each line to cover exactly the maximum possible points, and the lines must be disjoint in the points they cover. That suggests that a covering with all sunny lines is possible only if we can partition T_n into n sets, each set being the set of points on a line that is sunny and contains exactly (n+1)/2 points. For n odd, (n+1)/2 is integer. For n=5, (n+1)/2=3, we found two such lines: y=x and y=-2x+7. But they are not enough to partition 15 points into 5 triples. For n=5, we needed 5 triples, but only two triples exist. For n=7, we might have more such lines? Let's explore.

Thus the problem reduces to: For which k does there exist a set of n lines covering T_n, with exactly k of them being sunny, and the remaining n-k lines being non-sunny.

We need to find necessary conditions on k, and then show sufficiency by construction.

Let's analyze the covering with n lines. Since the total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2, and we have n lines, the sum of the number of points on each line (counting multiplicities) is at least N. But also each line can contain at most a certain number of points from T_n. Let's denote:

- For a non-sunny line (horizontal, vertical, slope -1), what is the maximum number of points from T_n it can contain? 
   * Horizontal line y = j: points (a,j) with a ≥ 1, a+j ≤ n+1 => a ≤ n+1-j. So number = n+1-j (for j from 1 to n). So maximum when j=1: n points. For j=n: 1 point.
   * Vertical line x = i: similarly number = n+1-i, maximum n points when i=1.
   * Slope -1 line y = -x + c: points with a+b=c, a,b≥1 => a from 1 to c-1, so number = c-1. Maximum when c=n+1: number = n. So each such line can contain up to n points. So the maximum for a non-sunny line is n points (for the line through the point (1,1) and (n,1) etc). Actually for y=1, it covers n points; for x=1, n points; for y=-x+(n+1), n points. So non-sunny lines can cover many points.

- For a sunny line, the maximum number of points is floor((n+1)/2) as argued (since slope 1 lines give that). But is it always exactly floor((n+1)/2)? Let's compute more generally. For a line with slope m ≠ 0,∞,-1, the number of integer lattice points in T_n is at most floor((n+1)/2) if we consider lines of slope 1? But could a line with slope, say, 2 have more? For n=6, floor((7)/2)=3, but slope2 gives at most 2. So slope1 is best. Could a line with slope, say, 1/2 have 3? For n=6, we saw max2. So the maximum is indeed floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, floor(6/2)=3; slope1 gave 3. For n=4, floor(5/2)=2; slope1 lines: y=x gives (1,1),(2,2) ->2 points; y=x+1 gives (1,2),(2,3) ->2; so max2. For n=3, floor(4/2)=2; slope1: y=x gives (1,1),(2,2) ->2; y=x+1 gives (1,2),(2,3) but (2,3) sum5>4? Actually n=3, n+1=4, (1,2) sum3, (2,3) sum5>4 so only (1,2) maybe? Wait, triangle for n=3: points with a+b≤4, a,b≥1: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). So slope1 y=x gives (1,1) only? (2,2) sum4, but (2,2) is not in triangle because a+b=4 ≤4, yes it is in triangle! So (2,2) is in triangle? For n=3, points: (1,1) sum2, (1,2) sum3, (2,1) sum3, (2,2) sum4, (3,1) sum4? Actually a+b≤4, so (2,2) sum4, (3,1) sum4, (1,3) sum4? (1,3) sum4, but (1,3) is y=3, x=1, sum4, so it's in triangle. So for n=3, points are: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 6 points. So slope1 y=x gives (1,1),(2,2) ->2 points. So max2. So the maximum for sunny lines is floor((n+1)/2). That seems consistent.

Thus we have: For a sunny line, the maximum number of points it can contain from T_n is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For a non-sunny line, it can contain up to n points. Since we need to cover all N points with n lines, we have a combinatorial bound. Let's denote the number of points on each line (counting multiplicities). Let s = number of sunny lines, and t = n-s non-sunny lines. Then total points covered (with multiplicity) is sum_{sunny} p_i + sum_{non-sunny} q_j ≥ N. Also each sunny p_i ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋, each non-sunny q_j ≤ n. So we have:

s·⌊(n+1)/2⌋ + t·n ≥ N = n(n+1)/2.

But we can also get a bound by considering that the sum of the maximum possible points on the lines is at most s·M + t·n, where M = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For the covering to be possible, this bound must be at least N. That gives a necessary condition on s. However, since the lines are distinct and the points are covered, it's possible that the sum of points on lines is greater than N due to overlaps, but we need at least N distinct points covered. So if the sum of the maximum possible points is less than N, then it's impossible to cover all points because even if we use the maximum points on each line, the total number of point incidences is at most that sum, but distinct points covered can be at most that sum; if that sum is less than N, we cannot cover N distinct points. So we require:

s·M + t·n ≥ N = n(n+1)/2.

Since t = n-s, this becomes:

s·M + (n-s)·n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

=> s·M + n^2 - s·n ≥ n^2/2 + n/2.

=> s·(M - n) ≥ n^2/2 + n/2 - n^2 = -n^2/2 + n/2.

=> s·(M - n) ≥ n/2 - n^2/2 = n(1-n)/2.

Since M = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋, we have M - n = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n. For n even, n=2m, then M = floor((2m+1)/2)=m, so M-n = m-2m = -m. For n odd, n=2m+1, then M = floor((2m+2)/2)=m+1, so M-n = (m+1)-(2m+1) = -m. So in both cases, M - n = -⌊n/2⌋. Actually for n=2m, floor(n/2)=m, so M-n = m-2m = -m = -floor(n/2). For n=2m+1, floor(n/2)=m, M-n = (m+1)-(2m+1) = -m = -floor(n/2). So M-n = -⌊n/2⌋.

Thus the inequality becomes:

s·(-⌊n/2⌋) ≥ n(1-n)/2.

Multiply both sides by -1 (reverse inequality):

s·⌊n/2⌋ ≤ n(n-1)/2.

=> s ≤ [n(n-1)/2] / ⌊n/2⌋.

Compute this bound.

Case n even: n=2m, then ⌊n/2⌋ = m. So s ≤ [2m(2m-1)/2] / m = [m(2m-1)] / m = 2m-1 = n-1.

Thus for n even, s ≤ n-1. So it's impossible to have s = n (all sunny lines). So k = n is impossible for even n. For n odd: n=2m+1, then ⌊n/2⌋ = m. So s ≤ [(2m+1)(2m)/2] / m = [(2m+1)m] / m = 2m+1 = n. So s ≤ n, which is not restrictive. So for odd n, it's possible that s = n (all sunny lines) in principle. However, we need to check if such a covering actually exists. For n=5 (odd), we found s=5 impossible because only two sunny triples exist. So the bound is necessary but not sufficient. For n=3 (odd), s=3 is possible (we gave example with three sunny lines). So for n=3, s=3 works. For n=7, maybe s=7 works? Need to analyze.

Thus the necessary condition from the sum bound is: s ≤ n for odd n, s ≤ n-1 for even n. So k cannot be n if n is even. So for even n, k cannot be n. For odd n, k could be n in principle, but we need to see if construction exists. For n=3, we have k=3; for n=5, we don't have k=5; so maybe for odd n ≥5, k=n is impossible. Need to check n=7.

Let's try to see if there is a covering with all sunny lines for n=7. Points: a,b≥1, a+b≤8. Number of points: 7*8/2=28. We need 7 sunny lines covering all 28 points. Each sunny line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2)=4 points (since (7+1)/2=4). The maximum total points if all lines have 4 points is 7*4=28, so we would need each line to contain exactly 4 points, and the sets of points on the lines must be disjoint (no overlap) because if any two lines share a point, the total distinct points covered would be less than 28, but we need 28 distinct points. So we would need a partition of T_7 into 7 collinear sets, each set being the set of points on a sunny line, each line containing exactly 4 points. So we need to find 7 distinct sunny lines, each containing exactly 4 points from T_7, and these 7 lines partition the 28 points.

Is that possible? We need to examine the structure of T_n. For n odd, the triangle has a central point? Similarly, y=x+1 gives (1,2),(2,3),(3,4),(4,5) -> 4 points? Check sum: (4,5) sum=9>8? Actually a+b=9>8, so (4,5) not in triangle; for a=4, b=5 gives sum9>8, so not included. So y=x+1: points (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) -> 3 points. So not 4. y=x-1 gives (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) -> 3 points. So only y=x gives 4 points. What about slope -2 lines? For n=7, slope -2: y=-2x+c. To have 4 points, need c such that there are 4 integer x≥1 with b≥1 and a+b≤8. Let's try c=9: points (1,7) sum8, (2,5) sum7, (3,3) sum6, (4,1) sum5 -> that's 4 points (since (1,7) sum8 ok, (2,5) sum7 ok, (3,3) sum6 ok, (4,1) sum5 ok). So y=-2x+9 gives 4 points. c=10: (1,8) sum9>8 no, (2,6) sum8 ok, (3,4) sum7 ok, (4,2) sum6 ok -> 3 points? Actually (2,6),(3,4),(4,2) are 3 points; (1,8) not. Let's search systematically for lines with slope m that are not 0,∞,-1 and contain 4 points from T_7.

We need a line y = mx + c (m rational) such that there are 4 integer a ≥ 1 with b = ma+c ≥ 1 and a+b ≤ 8.

The condition a+b = a(1+m)+c ≤ 8. For 4 points, we need a ranging over 4 consecutive integers, say a = a0, a0+1, a0+2, a0+3, such that each point lies in triangle. Also b must be integer and positive.

We can also consider lines with negative slope that give arithmetic progression of sums.

Alternatively, we can think in terms of the set of points with sum c. A line that is not horizontal/vertical/slope -1 will intersect each sum level at most once? Actually for a line with slope m, the sum a+b = a(1+m)+c. If m ≠ -1, then as a increases by 1, the sum increases by (1+m). So the sums are arithmetic progression with difference d = 1+m. For a line to contain 4 points, the sums of those points must be in arithmetic progression with difference d, and they must all be ≤ n+1. Also the points must have positive coordinates. So the line can be described by the arithmetic progression of sums.

For a given slope m, the number of points in T_n is the number of a such that a ≥ 1, ma+c ≥ 1, and a(1+m)+c ≤ n+1. This is essentially the length of the intersection of an arithmetic progression (with difference d) with the interval [2, n+1] after accounting for positivity constraints. The maximum number of points is achieved when d is small (since then we can have many steps within the bound). The smallest positive d is 1 (when m=0, but not sunny), d=0 (when m=-1, not sunny). For sunny lines, d can be 1? If m=0, slope0, not sunny. For m=1, d=2. For m=2, d=3. For m=-2, d=-1, absolute value 1? Actually d=1+m, so for m=-2, d=-1, but the absolute value of the step is 1. However, note that the sum decreases by 1 each step if m=-2. So the number of points is the number of consecutive sums from some lower bound to upper bound. For m=-2, d=-1, so sums decrease by 1 as a increases. That means if we have a line with slope -2, the points have sums that are consecutive integers. For n=7, sums range from 2 to 8. Thus for n=4, k=2 seems impossible. So our earlier assumption that all k are possible is false. So for n=4, possible k are 0,1,3,4? Let's test k=4 we have example: three sunny lines covering points and one extra sunny line. For n=4, we need 4 lines, exactly 4 sunny. Can we cover all points with 4 sunny lines? As we saw, sunny lines can have at most 2 points. With 4 lines, max distinct points = 8, but total points = 10, so impossible. Wait, we need to cover 10 points with 4 sunny lines. If each sunny line has at most 2 points, the total number of point incidences is at most 8, but distinct points covered can be at most 8, not enough for 10. So k=4 is impossible for n=4 because the sum bound earlier: for n even, s ≤ n-1, so s=4 not allowed. So k=4 impossible. So for n=4, possible k are 0,1,3? Let's verify k=3 we already have construction. k=2 we found impossible. k=0 and 1 are possible. So for n=4, possible k are 0,1,3.

Now for n=5, we found possible k=0,1,3. For n=6, we need to analyze.

Given the pattern, it seems that k=2 is always impossible for n≥4? But we saw for n=3, k=2 possible. For n=4, k=2 impossible. For n=5, k=2 impossible. For n=6, maybe also impossible? Let's try to see if there is a general necessary condition.

We need to determine all k such that there exist n lines covering T_n with exactly k sunny lines.

This is a combinatorial geometry problem. Let's try to find necessary conditions using double counting or parity.

Define the set of lines L = {L_1,...,L_n}. For each line L, let p(L) = number of points of T_n on L. Then sum_{L} p(L) ≥ N = n(n+1)/2. Also, for a sunny line L, p(L) ≤ M = floor((n+1)/2). For a non-sunny line L, p(L) ≤ n. Let s = number of sunny lines, t = n-s non-sunny lines. Then sum p(L) ≤ s·M + t·n. So we require s·M + (n-s)·n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

This gives s ≤ [n(n+1)/2 - n^2] / (n - M) = [n(n+1-2n)/2] / (n-M) = [n(1-n)/2] / (n-M). Since n-M = ⌊n/2⌋, we get s ≤ n(n-1)/(2⌊n/2⌋). For n even, n=2m, s ≤ (2m(2m-1))/(2m)=2m-1=n-1. For n odd, n=2m+1, s ≤ ((2m+1)(2m))/(2m)=2m+1=n. So the bound is s ≤ n for odd n, s ≤ n-1 for even n. So k = s cannot be n when n is even. For odd n, k could be n in principle, but we need to see if it's possible.

We already have examples: n=3 (odd) k=3 works; n=5 (odd) k=5 does not work (since only two sunny triples exist). So for odd n≥5, k=n is impossible. For n=7, we argued it's also impossible because only two sunny lines with 4 points exist, and the total points need 7 lines each with 4 points. But maybe we can have some sunny lines with 3 points and some with 4 points, and still cover all 28 points? Let's compute: For n=7, M=4, N=28. If we have s sunny lines, the maximum distinct points they can cover is at most sum of p(L) (since overlaps reduce distinct count). But we need at least 28 distinct points covered by the sunny lines. Since total points from sunny lines plus non-sunny lines must cover all points, we have to consider the possibility that non-sunny lines also cover many points. For k=7, all lines are sunny, so we need the 7 sunny lines to cover 28 distinct points. Each sunny line can have at most 4 points, so we need each to have exactly 4 points, and no overlaps. So we need a partition of T_7 into 7 collinear sets of 4 points each, each line sunny. Is that possible? Possibly using lines of slope 1, slope -2, etc., but we need 7 distinct lines. Could there be other sunny lines with 4 points? We found y=x and y=-2x+9. Are there others? Let's search systematically for lines with 4 points in T_7. We need a line y = mx + c (m rational) such that the intersection with T_7 has 4 integer points. The maximum sum is 8. For a line with slope m, the sums a+b = a(1+m)+c. For 4 points, we need a = a0, a0+1, a0+2, a0+3 such that each point lies in triangle. The sums are arithmetic progression with difference d = 1+m. The sums must be within [2,8] and also the points must have positive coordinates. For m=1, d=2, we got sums 2,4,6,8 which works. For m=-2, d=-1, we got sums 8,7,6,5 which works. For m=2, d=3, sums would be s, s+3, s+6, s+9; s+9 ≤8 impossible. For m=1/2, d=1.5, but then a must be even, so we can have a=2,4,6,8? That would be 4 points with sums 3+c, 6+c, 9+c, 12+c. For c chosen to make b positive, maybe? But also need sum ≤8: 12+c ≤8 => c≤-4, then b = a/2 + c for a=8 gives b=4+c≤0, so not positive. So no. For m=-1/2, d=0.5, but then a even? Actually for m=-1/2, b = -a/2 + c, integer requires a even. Then sums = a/2 + c. For a=2,4,6,8: sums = 1+c,2+c,3+c,4+c. Need ≤8: 4+c ≤8 => c≤4. Also b≥1: for a=2, b = -1+c ≥1 => c≥2; for a=4, b=-2+c ≥1 => c≥3; for a=6, b=-3+c ≥1 => c≥4; for a=8, b=-4+c ≥1 => c≥5. So if c=5, then a=8 gives b=1 ok, but a=2 gives b=4 ok, a=4 gives b=3 ok, a=6 gives b=2 ok? Actually check: a=2: b=-1+5=4 ok; a=4: b=-2+5=3 ok; a=6: b=-3+5=2 ok; a=8: b=-4+5=1 ok. So points: (2,4) sum6, (4,3) sum7, (6,2) sum8, (8,1) sum9? Wait sum for (8,1) is 9, but we need ≤8? Actually n+1=8, so sum must be ≤8. (8,1) sum=9>8, so not in triangle. So only a=2,4,6 give points? That's 3 points. So not 4. For c=4, a=8 gives b=0 no; a=6 gives b=1 sum7; a=4 gives b=2 sum6; a=2 gives b=3 sum5; so 3 points. So no 4.

Thus only two sunny lines with 4 points. So k=7 impossible for n=7. Similarly, for any odd n≥5, the maximum number of points on a sunny line is (n+1)/2, and we need to partition N = n(n+1)/2 points into n lines each with exactly (n+1)/2 points. Since only a few lines achieve that maximum, it's unlikely. But we need to prove that for odd n≥5, k=n is impossible. Possibly for n=5, we already have k=5 impossible. So for n odd, k=n is possible only for n=3. For n even, k=n is impossible by sum bound anyway. So the possible k are limited.

Now, we need to determine all k that are possible. From small n:

n=3: k=0,1,2,3 all possible.
n=4: k=0,1,3 possible? k=2? We didn't find. k=4? Not possible. So possible: 0,1,3.
n=5: k=0,1,3 possible? k=2? Not found. k=4? Not found. k=5? Not found. So 0,1,3.
n=6: Need to test. Let's attempt to find configurations for n=6.

Points: a,b≥1, a+b≤7. Total points: 21. We have 6 lines. Non-sunny lines max 6 points (vertical/horizontal/slope -1). Sunny lines max floor((n+1)/2)=3 points (since (6+1)/2=3.5 floor=3). So sunny lines can have at most 3 points. With 6 sunny lines, max distinct points = 6*3=18 <21, so k=6 impossible. By sum bound: for n even, s ≤ n-1 =5, so k=6 not allowed. So maximum k is 5. But is k=5 possible? Need 5 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line covering 21 points. The non-sunny line can have up to 6 points. The 5 sunny lines can have at most 3 each, total max 15, but they may overlap. To cover 21 points, the non-sunny line must cover many points, and the sunny lines must cover the rest, possibly with overlap. Since total distinct points needed is 21, and sum of max points is 6+15=21, if the non-sunny line has 6 points and the 5 sunny lines each have 3 points, and they are all disjoint, then union size =21. So we need a partition of T_6 into 1 set of 6 points (non-sunny line) and 5 sets of 3 points each (sunny lines). So we need to find 1 non-sunny line that contains exactly 6 points from T_6, and 5 sunny lines that each contain exactly 3 points, and these 6 lines partition the 21 points. Is that possible? For n=6, what are the non-sunny lines with 6 points? Horizontal y=1: points (1,1)-(6,1) -> 6 points (since 1+b≤7 => b≤6). y=2: points (1,2)-(5,2) -> 5 points; y=3: (1,3)-(4,3) ->4; y=4: (1,4)-(3,4) ->3; y=5: (1,5)-(2,5) ->2; y=6: (1,6) ->1. So y=1 gives 6 points. Vertical x=1: (1,1)-(1,6) ->6 points; x=2: (2,1)-(2,5) ->5; x=3: (3,1)-(3,4) ->4; x=4: (4,1)-(4,3) ->3; x=5: (5,1)-(5,2) ->2; x=6: (6,1) ->1. So x=1 gives 6. Slope -1 line y=-x+7: points (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) ->6 points. So there are three lines that cover 6 points: y=1, x=1, y=-x+7. These are all non-sunny. So we can choose one of them as the non-sunny line.

Now, we need 5 sunny lines, each with 3 points, that partition the remaining 15 points. For n=6, what are the sunny lines with 3 points? Maximum sunny points is 3. Let's find all sunny lines that contain exactly 3 points from T_6. We can search for lines with slope m not 0,∞,-1 that have 3 integer points in triangle. This is similar to finding lines with 3 points in the triangular grid. For n=6, the triangle points are:

Rows a=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(1,6)
a=2: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5)
a=3: (3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(3,4)
a=4: (4,1),(4,2),(4,3)
a=5: (5,1),(5,2)
a=6: (6,1)

We need to find all lines (sunny) that contain exactly 3 of these points. There are many possibilities. For instance, y=x gives points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) -> 3 points (since (4,4) sum=8>7 no). Now, we need to prove that for n≥4, k=2 is impossible, and k=3 is always possible, and also k=0,1 are possible trivially. In fact, these are the lines with slopes 1 and -2. The line with slope 1 gives points (a,a) with a from 1 to (n+1)/2. The line with slope -2 gives points (a, (n+1)/2 - a?) Actually y=-2x+c with c = n+1? For n=5, c=7 gave (1,5),(2,3),(3,1). For n=7, c=9 gave (2,5),(3,3),(4,1) -> 3 points, not 4. For n=9, c=11 gave 5 points. For n=7, we found no line with 4 points except y=x. So the only line with maximum points for n=7 is y=x. For n=9, we found y=x and y=-2x+11. So the number of maximum lines varies. But for n≥5, there are at most 2 such lines. So covering with n sunny lines is impossible because we would need n lines each with (n+1)/2 points, but we only have at most 2 such lines. More formally, we need to prove that for n≥4, the maximum number of points on a sunny line is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋, and there are at most 2 such lines. Actually we need to prove that for n≥5, there are at most 2 lines that contain exactly ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points from T_n. And if n is odd, then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = (n+1)/2, and we need n such lines, so impossible. If n is even, then the bound from sum is s ≤ n-1, so s=n is already impossible. So k=n is impossible for n≥4. For n=3, it's possible. So we have that.

Now, we need to show that for n≥4, k=2 is impossible. We have examples for n=4,5,6 where k=2 seems impossible. Need a general proof. Possibly by double counting or parity. 

Let's try to prove that for n≥4, it's impossible to have exactly 2 sunny lines covering T_n. Let L be the set of lines, with s=2 sunny lines, and t=n-2 non-sunny lines. The sum bound gives s·M + t·n ≥ N, with M = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For n even, M = n/2, so s·(n/2) + (n-2)·n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => 2·(n/2) + n(n-2) = n + n^2 -2n = n^2 - n ≥ n^2/2 + n/2? Actually N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2. So n^2 - n ≥ n^2/2 + n/2 => n^2/2 ≥ 3n/2 => n ≥ 3. So for n≥3, the sum bound is satisfied. For n odd, M = (n+1)/2, so s·(n+1)/2 + (n-2)·n = 2·(n+1)/2 + n(n-2) = (n+1) + n^2 -2n = n^2 - n +1. N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2. So we need n^2 - n +1 ≥ n^2/2 + n/2 => n^2/2 - 3n/2 +1 ≥0 => n^2 -3n +2 ≥0 => (n-1)(n-2)≥0, true for n≥2. So the sum bound does not rule out s=2 for any n≥3. So we need another argument.

Perhaps use that each sunny line can contain at most M points, and to cover all N points, the non-sunny lines must cover the remaining points. Since the non-sunny lines are at most n-2, they can cover at most (n-2)·n points, but we need to cover at least N - 2M points. This is possible. So we need a combinatorial geometry argument that the points in T_n cannot be covered by exactly 2 sunny lines and the rest non-sunny. 

Idea: Use the fact that the three families of non-sunny lines are "parallel" families. Consider the set of points with a+b = constant. A sunny line can intersect each such constant sum at most once. For a given constant c, the points on that diagonal are collinear on a line of slope -1. So non-sunny lines of slope -1 cover entire diagonals. Horizontal and vertical lines cover at most one point per diagonal? Actually a horizontal line y = j will intersect a diagonal a+b=c at points where a = c-j, so at most one point per diagonal. Similarly vertical line x=i gives at most one point per diagonal. So non-sunny lines can cover at most one point per diagonal, while slope -1 lines can cover an entire diagonal. Sunny lines can cover at most one point per diagonal as well.

Now, consider the diagonals. There are n+1 diagonals (c=2 to n+1). The total number of points is N = sum_{c=2}^{n+1} (c-1) = n(n+1)/2. The sunny lines cover at most M points each. We need to cover all points. The two sunny lines together cover at most 2M points. The remaining points must be covered by non-sunny lines. For each diagonal, the number of points covered by sunny lines is at most 2 (since each sunny line can hit a diagonal at most once, so 2 lines give at most 2 points per diagonal). The non-sunny lines must cover the rest. In particular, the diagonal with c=2 has only 1 point (1,1). If both sunny lines hit that diagonal? They could, but they might not. The point (1,1) must be covered by some line. If it is covered by a sunny line, then that sunny line contains (1,1). The other sunny line might also contain (1,1) if they intersect. But then that point is covered. Now, consider the diagonal with c=n+1. It has n points. Sunny lines can cover at most 2 points on that diagonal. The remaining n-2 points must be covered by non-sunny lines. Since non-sunny lines can cover at most one point per diagonal (unless they are slope -1 lines, which can cover entire diagonal). So to cover the n-2 points on that diagonal, we need at least n-2 non-sunny lines that are not slope -1? Actually slope -1 lines can cover an entire diagonal, so if we use a slope -1 line as non-sunny, it covers all points on that diagonal. That would be efficient. So the non-sunny lines might include the diagonal line y = -x + (n+1) as one line. That line covers all n points on that diagonal. So the remaining points on that diagonal are covered. So the two sunny lines could cover some points on other diagonals, and the non-sunny lines include the diagonal lines for some diagonals. So we need to show that it's impossible to have exactly 2 sunny lines.

Perhaps use a counting argument with the sum of coordinates modulo something. Let's consider the sum of coordinates of all points. That sum is sum_{a=1}^{n} sum_{b=1}^{n+1-a} (a+b). This sum is something. For each line, we can compute the sum of coordinates of points on it. Then sum over lines of sum of coordinates on each line is at least the sum of all coordinates (since each point is counted at least once). But we can also bound this sum by the maximum possible sum per line times number of lines. This might give a restriction. 

Let S = sum_{(a,b)∈T_n} (a+b) = ∑_{a=1}^{n} ∑_{b=1}^{n+1-a} (a+b) = ∑_{a=1}^{n} ( (a+b) count) but we can compute: S = ∑_{a=1}^{n} (a * (n+1-a) + ∑_{b=1}^{n+1-a} b ) = ∑_{a=1}^{n} ( a(n+1-a) + (n+1-a)(n+2-a)/2 ) = ∑_{a=1}^{n} ( (n+1-a)(a + (n+2-a)/2) ) = ∑_{a=1}^{n} ( (n+1-a)( (n+2)/2 ) ) = (n+2)/2 ∑_{a=1}^{n} (n+1-a) = (n+2)/2 * n(n+1)/2 = n(n+1)(n+2)/4. So the sum of all coordinates is n(n+1)(n+2)/4.

Now, for a line L, let p(L) = number of points on L, and let S(L) = sum of coordinates of points on L. Then ∑_{L} S(L) ≥ S. Also, we can compute maximum possible S(L) for a line. For a given line, the points are on a line. For a line with slope m (not 0,∞,-1), the coordinates (a, ma+c) integer. The sum of coordinates is a + ma + c = (1+m)a + c. Over a set of a values, the sum is (1+m)∑a + c·p. For a sunny line, we can have p ≤ M, and the sum can be large. But we can bound S(L) by the maximum sum of coordinates on any line with at most M points? Actually we can find an upper bound for S(L) for a line with p points from T_n. Since the points lie in the triangle, the maximum sum of coordinates on a line is achieved when the line goes through points with large coordinates. For a line that is not vertical/horizontal/slope -1, the sum of coordinates of its points is at most something like (n+1) + (n+1) = 2(n+1) if it contains only two points? But we need a general bound.

Perhaps easier: Show that for n≥4, any covering of T_n by n lines must contain at least one non-sunny line. That is, k cannot be n. That we already have: for n even, bound gives s≤n-1; for n odd, we need to show s=n impossible. So k=n is impossible for n≥4. That's one part.

Now, for k=2, we need to show impossibility for n≥4. Let's try to prove by contradiction: Suppose there exist n lines, exactly 2 sunny, covering T_n. Let the sunny lines be L1 and L2. Consider the set of points not covered by the sunny lines. Since sunny lines can cover at most M points each, they cover at most 2M points. The remaining points, at least N - 2M, must be covered by the n-2 non-sunny lines. But non-sunny lines can cover at most n points each (for slope -1 lines) or at most something else. However, we need to consider that the non-sunny lines may have overlaps, but we need to cover all points. Perhaps we can use the concept of "sum of coordinates" on each diagonal. Let's denote the diagonals D_c = {(a,b): a+b=c, a,b≥1, c≤n+1}. For each diagonal c, the number of points is c-1. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points on each diagonal (since each sunny line can intersect a diagonal at most once). The non-sunny lines: horizontal and vertical lines can cover at most 1 point per diagonal; slope -1 lines can cover all points on a diagonal. So if we have t non-sunny lines, they can cover at most t diagonals entirely (if they are slope -1 lines), and they can cover at most t points on the remaining diagonals (if they are horizontal/vertical). To cover the points on a diagonal, we need enough coverage.

Now, the two sunny lines together can cover at most 2 points on each diagonal. So for a diagonal with c-1 points, we need to cover the remaining c-3 points. If c-1 ≤ 2, then sunny lines may cover all points, but for c=2, only 1 point, so that point must be covered by some line. It could be covered by a sunny line or by a non-sunny line. If it is covered by a sunny line, then that diagonal is fully covered. For c=3, there are 2 points. Sunny lines can cover at most 2 points, so they could cover both. So all diagonals with few points may be fully covered by sunny lines. For larger diagonals, we need non-sunny lines to cover the bulk. 

But we have only n-2 non-sunny lines. The maximum number of points that non-sunny lines can cover on a diagonal is: if we use a slope -1 line, it covers all points on that diagonal; if we use horizontal/vertical, it covers at most 1 point per diagonal. So to cover the points on a diagonal with many points, we would want to use a slope -1 line for that diagonal. However, there are at most n-2 such lines, and there are n diagonals (c=2..n+1). So we can cover at most n-2 diagonals entirely. The remaining diagonals must be covered by horizontal/vertical lines, each covering at most 1 point per diagonal. But the number of points on those diagonals may be large, so we would need many horizontal/vertical lines to cover them, but we have only n-2 non-sunny lines total. If we use some slope -1 lines to cover some diagonals, the other diagonals must be covered by horizontal/vertical lines, each covering at most 1 point per diagonal. However, for a diagonal with c-1 points, if it is not covered by a slope -1 line, then the sunny lines cover at most 2 points, so at least c-3 points remain. Those must be covered by horizontal/vertical lines. Since each such line can cover at most 1 point per diagonal, we need at least c-3 such lines for that diagonal. But we have only n-2 non-sunny lines total. This seems impossible for large diagonals. 

Let's make this precise. For a given diagonal D_c (c from 2 to n+1), let r_c be the number of points on D_c that are not covered by the sunny lines. Since sunny lines can cover at most 2 points on D_c, we have r_c ≥ c-1 - 2 = c-3. For c≥4, c-3 ≥1. So r_c ≥ c-3. Now, the non-sunny lines must cover these r_c points. A non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point on D_c unless it is the slope -1 line for that diagonal, in which case it covers all c-1 points. But if we use a slope -1 line for D_c, then it covers all points on D_c, so r_c = 0. So for a diagonal that is not covered by a slope -1 line, the non-sunny lines covering its points must be horizontal/vertical lines, each covering at most 1 point. Thus we need at least r_c distinct non-sunny lines to cover the points on D_c. Since the non-sunny lines are shared across diagonals, we need to count the total number of non-sunny lines required to cover the points on all diagonals not fully covered by slope -1 lines. 

We have t = n-2 non-sunny lines. Let's denote the set of diagonals that are fully covered by slope -1 lines. Suppose we have k such diagonals. Then those diagonals have their points covered by one line each. The remaining n+1 - k diagonals are not fully covered by a slope -1 line, so they must be covered by horizontal/vertical lines. On each such diagonal D_c, the number of points that need to be covered by horizontal/vertical lines is at least c-3 (since sunny lines cover at most 2). For c≥4, c-3 ≥1. So we need at least ∑_{c in remaining} (c-3) distinct horizontal/vertical lines? But a single horizontal/vertical line can cover at most one point on a given diagonal, but it can cover points on multiple diagonals. However, for a diagonal, each point is on a different vertical/horizontal line? Actually a point (a,b) lies on a unique vertical line x=a and a unique horizontal line y=b. So covering a point by a horizontal/vertical line uses that line. A single horizontal line y = j can cover at most one point per diagonal? It can cover points on many diagonals, but for a given diagonal D_c, the point on D_c with y=j is (c-j, j) if c-j≥1. So a horizontal line can cover at most one point on each diagonal. So for a diagonal D_c, we need at least c-3 distinct horizontal/vertical lines to cover its points, but a horizontal line might cover a point on D_c and also a point on another diagonal. So the total number of horizontal/vertical lines needed to cover all points on the remaining diagonals is at least the maximum over diagonals of (c-3) maybe, but we need to consider that a line can cover points on different diagonals. However, we have only t = n-2 non-sunny lines total. If we have many diagonals, the total points to cover is large, so we would need many lines. 

But we can choose which diagonals are covered by slope -1 lines. Since we have only n-2 non-sunny lines, we can cover at most n-2 diagonals entirely. The remaining diagonals (at least n+1 - (n-2) = 3 diagonals) must be covered by horizontal/vertical lines. Among those, the diagonal with c=n+1 has n points, and sunny lines cover at most 2, so at least n-2 points remain. Those must be covered by horizontal/vertical lines. Each such line can cover at most 1 point on that diagonal (since it can cover at most one point on a diagonal unless it is the slope -1 line, which we are not using). So we need at least n-2 distinct horizontal/vertical lines to cover that diagonal. But we have only n-2 non-sunny lines total, so all non-sunny lines must be used to cover that diagonal. Moreover, those lines must be horizontal or vertical, because slope -1 lines would cover the entire diagonal, but we are not using them. So all t = n-2 lines must be horizontal or vertical, and they must cover the n-2 points on the diagonal D_{n+1} that are not covered by sunny lines. But each such line covers exactly one point on D_{n+1} (since a line x=i hits D_{n+1} at (i, n+1-i) if i from 1 to n). So we need n-2 distinct vertical lines or horizontal lines. However, we also need to cover points on other diagonals. The same horizontal/vertical lines will also cover points on other diagonals. But we must ensure that all points on the other diagonals are covered by the sunny lines or by these lines. 

Now, the diagonal D_{n+1} has n points. The two sunny lines can cover at most 2 of them. So at least n-2 points remain. They must be covered by the n-2 non-sunny lines, each covering one point on D_{n+1}. So those non-sunny lines are exactly the lines that pass through those points. They are either vertical lines x=i or horizontal lines y=j. Each such line also covers points on other diagonals. In particular, for a point (i, n+1-i) on D_{n+1}, the vertical line x=i will also cover points (i, b) for b from 1 to n+1-i? Actually the vertical line x=i contains all points with x=i, which in T_n are (i,b) with b from 1 to n+1-i. So it covers the entire column i. Similarly, a horizontal line y=j contains all points with y=j, i.e., (a,j) with a from 1 to n+1-j. So these lines cover many points.

Now, we need to cover all points on all diagonals. The points on D_{n+1} are covered by the n-2 lines (one each). The points on other diagonals must be covered by either the sunny lines or these lines. Let's see if we can choose the two sunny lines to cover all remaining points. Since the non-sunny lines cover entire columns or rows, they cover many points. The question reduces to: Is there a selection of two sunny lines (each with at most M points) and a set of n-2 vertical/horizontal lines that cover all points? Possibly we can choose the non-sunny lines to be the columns corresponding to the points on D_{n+1} not covered by sunny lines, but we need to also cover points that are not on D_{n+1}. For n=4, we tried and it didn't work. 

Let's try to formulate a general proof. Suppose there are exactly 2 sunny lines L1 and L2. Let their points be P1 and P2. Consider the set of points not on L1 or L2. Since L1 and L2 are sunny, they cannot be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. So they intersect each diagonal at most once. For a given diagonal c, the points on that diagonal that are not on L1 or L2 are at least c-1 - 2 = c-3. For c = n+1, we have at least n-2 points. These must be covered by non-sunny lines. The only way to cover n-2 points on a diagonal is to use n-2 lines, each covering exactly one point on that diagonal (since a slope -1 line would cover all n points, but then we would have a slope -1 non-sunny line, which we could use, but then we wouldn't need all n-2 lines). Actually we could use a slope -1 line to cover the whole diagonal, but that would be a non-sunny line. That would be efficient. So why not use a slope -1 line for D_{n+1}? That would cover all n points, and then we wouldn't need n-2 lines. But we have only n-2 non-sunny lines total. If we use a slope -1 line for D_{n+1}, that's 1 line, and then we need to cover the rest of points with the remaining n-3 lines. That might be possible. So the two sunny lines could be such that they cover some points on D_{n+1}, but we don't have to cover the entire diagonal with horizontal/vertical lines; we could use a slope -1 line for that diagonal. But we have only n-2 non-sunny lines, so we could use at most n-2 slope -1 lines, but there are n diagonals, so we could cover up to n-2 diagonals entirely with slope -1 lines. That would leave 3 diagonals uncovered by slope -1 lines. Those 3 diagonals must be covered by horizontal/vertical lines. In particular, the diagonal with c=n+1 might be covered by a slope -1 line, reducing the need for horizontal/vertical lines. So the argument about needing n-2 horizontal/vertical lines only applies if we do not use a slope -1 line for D_{n+1}. But we can choose to use a slope -1 line for that diagonal. So we need to consider the optimal selection.

Thus to show impossibility, we need to show that no matter how we assign the non-sunny lines (which can be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1), the points cannot all be covered by exactly 2 sunny lines. This seems like a combinatorial geometry problem that might require more intricate counting.

Given the pattern, and the fact that we have explicit constructions for n=4,5,6 with k=3, and we have not found any with k=2, I'm leaning that the answer is: For n=3, k=0,1,2,3; for n≥4, k=0,1,3. But we need to also consider k=4 for n=4? Not possible. For n=5, k=4? Not possible. For n=6, k=4? Not possible. So only 0,1,3.

Now, we need to provide a rigorous proof. Let's try to prove that for n≥4, k=2 is impossible. 

Let’s denote the three families of non-sunny lines: H (horizontal), V (vertical), D (slope -1). Let s=2 sunny lines. Let t = n-2 non-sunny lines. For each line L, let p(L) = number of points on L from T_n. We have ∑ p(L) ≥ N = n(n+1)/2. Also, for a sunny line, p(L) ≤ M = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For a non-sunny line, p(L) ≤ n (if it is a full column/row/diagonal). So ∑ p(L) ≤ 2M + t·n. This gives 2M + (n-2)n ≥ n(n+1)/2. For n even, M = n/2, so n + n(n-2) = n^2 - n ≥ n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2 => n^2 - n ≥ n^2/2 + n/2 => n^2/2 ≥ 3n/2 => n ≥ 3, holds. For n odd, M = (n+1)/2, so 2·(n+1)/2 + n(n-2) = n+1 + n^2 -2n = n^2 - n +1. N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2+n)/2. So inequality n^2 - n +1 ≥ (n^2+n)/2 => 2n^2 -2n+2 ≥ n^2+n => n^2 -3n+2 ≥0 => (n-1)(n-2)≥0, true. So sum bound doesn't rule out.

Now, consider the set of points on the diagonal D_{n+1}. It has n points. Let the two sunny lines cover at most 2 of these points. So at least n-2 points on D_{n+1} must be covered by non-sunny lines. A non-sunny line can cover at most one point on D_{n+1} unless it is the slope -1 line for that diagonal, which would cover all n points. But if we use a slope -1 line for D_{n+1}, then that line covers all n points, so the points on D_{n+1} are fully covered by that single line, and we don't need to cover them by other non-sunny lines. So the two sunny lines could cover some points on D_{n+1}, and the non-sunny lines could include the slope -1 line for D_{n+1} or not. If we include that slope -1 line, then the points on D_{n+1} are covered, and we have n-3 non-sunny lines left. Those n-3 lines must cover the remaining points (on other diagonals). So we need to consider two cases:

Case 1: There is a non-sunny line that is the slope -1 line for D_{n+1}. Then that line covers all n points on D_{n+1}. So the two sunny lines can cover at most 2 points on that diagonal, but those points are already covered, so it's fine. Now, the remaining points are on diagonals D_2,...,D_n. So the total capacity of non-sunny lines (each at most n points) is (n-3)n. So the total capacity of all lines is 2M + (n-3)n. This must be at least n(n-1)/2. Compute: 2M + n(n-3). For n even, M=n/2, so n + n^2 -3n = n^2 -2n. n(n-1)/2 = (n^2-n)/2. So n^2-2n ≥ (n^2-n)/2 => 2n^2-4n ≥ n^2-n => n^2 -3n ≥0 => n≥3. For n odd, M=(n+1)/2, so 2·(n+1)/2 + n(n-3) = n+1 + n^2-3n = n^2 -2n+1. N_remaining = n(n-1)/2 = (n^2-n)/2. So n^2-2n+1 ≥ (n^2-n)/2 => 2n^2-4n+2 ≥ n^2-n => n^2 -3n+2 ≥0 => (n-1)(n-2)≥0, true. So capacity not an issue. But we need to see if it's possible to cover all points with these lines. The diagonals D_2,...,D_n have at most n-1 points each. The sunny lines can cover at most 1 point per diagonal, so they cover at most 2 points per diagonal. The non-sunny lines can cover at most 1 point per diagonal unless they are slope -1 lines for that diagonal. But we have only n-3 non-sunny lines left. We could use some of them as slope -1 lines for some diagonals. However, there are n-1 diagonals (D_2..D_n). So we cannot cover all diagonals with slope -1 lines. Some diagonals will have to be covered by horizontal/vertical lines. For a diagonal with many points, say D_n has n-1 points, and the two sunny lines cover at most 2, so at least n-3 points remain. But we only have n-3 non-sunny lines total (including the one used for D_{n+1}? Actually we have n-3 non-sunny lines left, but they could be used as slope -1 lines for other diagonals. If we use a slope -1 line for D_n, then it covers all n-1 points, so the points on D_n are covered by one line. But then we have n-4 lines left for other diagonals. So we can choose which diagonals get slope -1 lines. The key is to show that we cannot cover all points with exactly 2 sunny lines.

Given the time, I think it's safe to conclude the answer is as conjectured. Since the problem asks to determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both conditions, we can present the solution as:

- If n=3, then k can be 0,1,2,3.
- If n≥4, then k can be 0,1,3.

We should also provide explicit constructions for each case.

Another approach: Use the concept of "sum of coordinates" modulo something. Consider the sum of the coordinates of all points on the diagonal D_c. Let's formalize: Let the set of diagonals be C = {2,3,...,n+1}. For each c, let d_c = c-1 be the number of points on that diagonal. The two sunny lines cover at most 2 points on each diagonal. Now, a non-sunny line can be of three types:
- Vertical: x=i. It covers points (i,b) for b=1..n+1-i. On a given diagonal c, it covers at most 1 point (specifically, if c-i≥1 and c-i≤n+1-i, then it covers point (i, c-i)). So a vertical line contributes to at most 1 point per diagonal.
- Horizontal: y=j. Similar.
- Slope -1: y=-x+c. It covers all points on that diagonal, i.e., d_c points.

Thus, for a diagonal c, the total number of points that can be covered by a single non-sunny line is: if we use the slope -1 line for that diagonal, it covers all d_c points; otherwise, each vertical/horizontal line covers at most 1 point on that diagonal.

So they could cover both points. D_2 has 1 point. That point could be covered by one of the sunny lines if it lies on that line. So we need to see if there exist two sunny lines L1 and L2 that together cover the points on D_2 and D_3. Points on D_2: (1,1). Points on D_3: (1,2) and (2,1). So we need L1 and L2 to cover these 3 points. Is that possible? L1 and L2 are sunny lines, so they cannot be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. They must contain these points. For example, consider L1: y = 2x -1? This passes through (1,1) and (2,3) not. (1,2) gives 2=2-1=1 no. L1: y = x+? For (1,2) and (2,1) slope -1, not allowed. (1,1) and (2,1) horizontal, not allowed. (1,2) and (1,1) vertical, not allowed. So no single sunny line can contain two of these points. The only possible way to cover all three points with two sunny lines is to have each line contain at most one of the points, and together they cover all three. That is possible if, for example, L1 contains (1,1) and L2 contains (1,2) and (2,1) but L2 would then need to contain both (1,2) and (2,1), which are on a line of slope -1, not sunny. So L2 cannot contain both. So the only possibility is: one sunny line contains (1,1) and the other contains (1,2) and (2,1)? Not possible. So we cannot cover all three points with two sunny lines. Thus the assignment where we cover D_4..D_{n+1} with slope -1 lines leaves D_2 and D_3 uncovered, and we have no vertical/horizontal lines left. The two sunny lines cannot cover all three points. So this fails.

What if we assign slope -1 lines to some other diagonals, not necessarily the largest? We want to minimize the number of points that need to be covered by vertical/horizontal lines. Since the points on large diagonals are many, we would want to cover them with slope -1 lines. The best is to cover the largest diagonals, as we did. That leaves the smallest diagonals with few points. But we still need to cover those points. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points per diagonal. For the small diagonals, we can use sunny lines to cover some points. However, the points on D_2 and D_3 must be covered. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points on D_3, but they cannot cover both (1,2) and (2,1) because that line is slope -1. So the two sunny lines can cover at most 1 point on D_3 (since if they both hit D_3, they would cover at most 2 distinct points, but they cannot cover both because that would require a slope -1 line). So they can cover at most 1 point on D_3. The point (1,1) on D_2 could be covered by a sunny line, but then we have 2 points left: (1,2) and (2,1). Since we have no vertical/horizontal lines if we used all non-sunny lines as slope -1 lines, this fails. So we need to use at least one vertical/horizontal line. That means we must not use all n-2 non-sunny lines as slope -1 lines. So we need to use some vertical/horizontal lines. That reduces the number of slope -1 lines we can have. But then the points on large diagonals that are not covered by slope -1 lines must be covered by vertical/horizontal lines. Since we have only t - k vertical/horizontal lines, and each can cover at most 1 point per diagonal, the total points they can cover is limited. We need to show that no matter how we assign the t lines, it's impossible to cover all points with exactly 2 sunny lines.

Let's try to set up an inequality. Let the set of diagonals be C = {2,3,...,n+1}. For each diagonal c, let a_c be the number of points on diagonal c that are covered by non-sunny lines. Thus for n=5, it's impossible to have exactly 2 sunny lines. So the argument that we need at least one vertical/horizontal line is not sufficient to rule out k=2, but we can use a more precise counting.

Given the pattern, I think the correct answer is: for n=3, k=0,1,2,3; for n≥4, k=0,1,3. We need to provide a proof. I'll attempt to write a rigorous proof.

Proof outline:

Let T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a+b ≤ n+1}. Let L be a set of n distinct lines covering T_n. For a line L, define its type: H (horizontal), V (vertical), D (slope -1), or S (sunny, i.e., not H,V,D). Let s = number of sunny lines, t = n-s non-sunny lines.

We have sum_{L} |L ∩ T_n| ≥ |T_n| = N = n(n+1)/2.

Also, for a sunny line, |L ∩ T_n| ≤ M = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

We will prove that for n ≥ 4, s cannot be 2. 

Consider the diagonal D_{n+1} (points with a+b = n+1). It has n points. For any sunny line, it intersects D_{n+1} in at most 1 point. So the total number of points on D_{n+1} covered by sunny lines is at most s. Hence the number of points on D_{n+1} that must be covered by non-sunny lines is at least n - s. 

Now, consider the set of non-sunny lines. They are of three types: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. A horizontal line y=j intersects D_{n+1} in at most 1 point (specifically, (n+1-j, j) if j ≤ n). A vertical line x=i intersects D_{n+1} in at most 1 point (i, n+1-i). A slope -1 line y=-x+c intersects D_{n+1} in either 0 or all n points (if c = n+1). So a non-sunny line can cover all n points of D_{n+1} only if it is the line y=-x+(n+1). Otherwise, it covers at most 1 point. Therefore, if we want to cover the n - s points on D_{n+1} that are not covered by sunny lines, we have two options: either we use the slope -1 line for D_{n+1} (which covers all n points), or we use horizontal/vertical lines, each covering at most 1 point, so we need at least n - s such lines. Since we have only t = n - s non-sunny lines, the number of horizontal/vertical lines we can have is at most t. If we use the slope -1 line, then we have t-1 lines left for other diagonals. If we don't use it, then we need at least n - s horizontal/vertical lines to cover D_{n+1}, which forces t ≥ n - s, i.e., t ≥ n - s, which is true because t = n - s. So it's possible that all non-sunny lines are used as horizontal/vertical lines to cover D_{n+1}. 

Now, consider the diagonal D_{n} (points with a+b = n). It has n-1 points. The sunny lines cover at most s points on D_n. The remaining points on D_n must be covered by non-sunny lines. A non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point on D_n unless it is the slope -1 line for D_n (which would cover all n-1 points). So if we want to cover all points on D_n that are not covered by sunny lines, we need either to use the slope -1 line for D_n (which covers all points on D_n), or use enough horizontal/vertical lines to cover the points. Since we have limited horizontal/vertical lines, we need to see if it's possible.

We will use a counting argument on the total number of points on diagonals with small d_c. 

Let’s denote by p_c = c-1 the number of points on diagonal D_c. For c ≤ 3, p_c = 1 or 2. The points on D_2 and D_3 are 3 points. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 of these points (since on D_3, they can cover at most 1 point because covering both would require a slope -1 line). So at least 1 point on D_2∪D_3 must be covered by non-sunny lines. Similarly, on D_4, there are 3 points, and the sunny lines can cover at most 2 (since they cannot cover all 3). So at least 1 point on D_4 must be covered by non-sunny lines. For larger c, the sunny lines can cover at most 2 points, but the number of points on the diagonal is larger, so many points must be covered by non-sunny lines. 

We can sum the required coverage from non-sunny lines over all diagonals. For a diagonal D_c, let b_c be the number of points on D_c covered by sunny lines. Then b_c ≤ 2. The number of points that need to be covered by non-sunny lines is p_c - b_c ≥ p_c - 2 = c-3. So the total number of points that must be covered by non-sunny lines is at least ∑_{c=4}^{n+1} (c-3) = (n-2)(n-1)/2. Let’s denote R = (n-2)(n-1)/2. 

Now, consider the coverage provided by non-sunny lines. Let k be the number of diagonals that have a slope -1 line among the non-sunny lines. For each such diagonal c, the slope -1 line covers all p_c points. For the other diagonals, each point must be covered by vertical/horizontal lines. A vertical line x=i covers at most 1 point per diagonal, and the total number of points it can cover across all diagonals is at most n (since it covers points with x=i, which for c from i+1 to n+1? Actually x=i covers points (i,b) for b=1..n+1-i, so it covers points on diagonals c = i+1, i+2, ..., n+1? Because a+b=c => b=c-i, and b≥1 => c≥i+1; b≤n+1-i => c≤n+1. So x=i covers points on diagonals c = i+1,...,n+1. So it covers exactly (n+1-i) diagonals. So each vertical line covers at most n points (when i=1). Similarly, a horizontal line y=j covers points on diagonals c = j+1,...,n+1, covering at most n points. So the total number of points that vertical/horizontal lines can cover is at most (t-k) * n. 

Therefore, the total number of points that non-sunny lines can cover is at most S_k + (t-k)n, where S_k = sum of p_c over the k diagonals that have slope -1 lines. To maximize coverage, we would assign slope -1 lines to diagonals with the largest p_c. We need to solve a combinatorial geometry problem. Let me restate:

We have n ≥ 3 integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:
1. For all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.
2. Exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal (parallel to x-axis), not vertical (parallel to y-axis), and not parallel to x+y=0 (i.e., slope -1). So sunny lines have slope not equal to 0, ∞, or -1. They can have any other slope.

We need to find all k for which such a configuration exists.

We have n distinct lines. They must cover all integer lattice points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1. So points: (1,1), (1,2), ..., (1,n), (2,1), (2,2), ..., (2,n-1), ... up to (n,1). That's a triangular set of points. Number of points: sum_{i=1}^{n} i = n(n+1)/2. But note: (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1, a,b positive integers. So indeed points (1,1) to (n,1) etc.

We need to assign each such point to at least one line. The lines are distinct. Exactly k of them are sunny.

Goal: Determine all possible k (nonnegative integers) for which there exist n lines satisfying coverage condition.

Interpretation: We need to find necessary conditions on k and sufficiency by construction.

Observations: Since there are n lines, they can cover many points. The condition is that each of the triangular points lies on at least one line. The lines can be arbitrary, but they must be distinct.

Let’s denote the set of lines L = {L_1,...,L_n}. For each line, it contains some of these points. The coverage condition is that the union of the sets of points on each line includes all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1.

We need to count how many of these lines can be sunny. Sunny lines are those with slope not 0, ∞, -1.

We need to determine possible k.

That's three lines, but none are sunny. So k=0. Could we have k=1? We need exactly one sunny line. We can adjust: Use two non-sunny lines to cover some points, and one sunny line covering the rest. But we need to cover all three points. Possibly: L1: vertical x=1 (non-sunny) covers (1,1),(1,2). L2: sunny line through (2,1) with slope 2, covers (2,1). L3: horizontal y=1 (non-sunny) covers (1,1),(2,1). But then (1,2) is covered by L1; (2,1) by L2 and L3; (1,1) by L1 and L3. That's three lines: one sunny, two non-sunny. So k=1 works. Could k=0? Already example. So for n=3, all k=0,1,2,3? Wait we need exactly k sunny lines, not at most. For k=2: we need two sunny lines. Can we have two sunny lines and one non-sunny covering all three points? Possibly: L1: vertical x=1 (non-sunny) covers (1,1),(1,2). L2: sunny line through (2,1) with slope 2. L3: sunny line through (1,2) with slope 3? But then (1,2) is covered by L1, so L3 could be any line through (1,2) that is sunny; but we need distinct lines. However, if we have L1 non-sunny, L2 sunny, L3 sunny, then (1,1) is covered by L1; (1,2) by L1; (2,1) by L2. That works. So k=2 works. So for n=3, all k from 0 to 3 are possible? But check k=0: we gave three non-sunny lines. That's fine. So for n=3, k can be 0,1,2,3.

Now for general n. Let's think about constraints.

First, note that the points are all in the first quadrant. The lines can be any distinct lines. The coverage condition is that each of the points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1 lies on at least one line.

We have n lines. The maximum number of lines that can be sunny? Could it be n? Possibly yes, by constructing lines each through a single point? But there are many points, n lines cannot cover all points if each covers only one point, because we have n(n+1)/2 points. So each line must cover many points. But we could have lines that cover many points and are sunny. For n large, we need to cover triangular array of points. We have n lines. Possibly we can use lines of the form y = x + c? But those have slope 1, not -1. So they are sunny. Also lines with other slopes.

Goal: Determine all k such that there exist n lines covering the points and exactly k of them are sunny.

Observations: Non-sunny lines are those that are horizontal (parallel to x-axis), vertical (parallel to y-axis), or slope -1 (parallel to x+y=0). These are three families of lines.

If we have a non-sunny line, it might cover many points. For instance, vertical line x = i covers points (i,b) for b≥1 and i+b ≤ n+1 => b ≤ n+1-i. So number of points covered: n+1-i (since b from 1 to n+1-i). Similarly horizontal line y = j covers (a,j) with a from 1 to n+1-j. Slope -1 lines: y = -x + c. They cover points where a,b positive, a+b ≤ n+1 and b = -a + c => c = a+b. So c must be at least 2 (since a,b≥1) and at most n+1. For a given c, the points are (a, c-a) with a from max(1, c-n) to min(n, c-1). But since a,b≥1, a≥1, c-a≥1 => a ≤ c-1, a ≥ c-n. So number of points: if c ≤ n+1? Actually c can be from 2 to n+1. For c=2: points (1,1). For c=3: (1,2),(2,1). etc. So a line of slope -1 with intercept c covers points with a+b=c. So it covers exactly one point for c=2, two for c=3, ... up to n points for c=n+1? Wait a+b=c, a,b≥1 => a from 1 to c-1, so number of integer points = c-1. But if c ≤ n+1, then all these points satisfy a+b ≤ n+1 because c ≤ n+1. So yes, the line y = -x + c covers exactly c-1 points from the triangle. For c=n+1, it covers n points (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). So slope -1 lines cover many points.

Now, we have n lines total. They must cover all points in the triangle. So we can think of covering these points with n lines. This is reminiscent of covering a triangular grid with lines. The maximum number of lines needed to cover all points? Possibly we can use lines of the form x = constant for each column? But then we have n columns? Actually points (a,b) with a from 1 to n, b from 1 to n+1-a. So there are n columns a=1,...,n, but column a has n+1-a points. Similarly rows. So if we take vertical lines x = i for i=1,...,n, they cover all points. That's n lines, all vertical (non-sunny). So that gives k=0. Similarly horizontal lines y = j for j=1,...,n cover all points? Check: y=j covers points (a,j) with a from 1 to n+1-j. For j=1, a=1..n; j=2, a=1..n-1; ... j=n, a=1; j=n+1? But j positive integer, and a+b ≤ n+1, so y=j is possible only if j ≤ n+1? Actually j can be up to n+1? For j=n+1, then a+b ≤ n+1 and b=n+1 => a≤0, no points. So j from 1 to n. That gives n lines. So horizontal lines also give k=0. Slope -1 lines: y = -x + c for c=2,...,n+1 give n lines? Actually c from 2 to n+1 gives n lines, but each covers c-1 points; the union of all such lines covers all points because each point has a+b=c, so it lies on the line y=-x+c. So the n lines y = -x+2, y = -x+3, ..., y = -x+(n+1) cover all points. Are they distinct? Yes. So that's also n lines, all slope -1 (non-sunny). So we have three families of n lines each that cover all points, and each line is non-sunny. So k=0 is achievable.

Now, can we have k = n? That would mean all lines are sunny. Is it possible to cover all triangular points with n sunny lines? We need to find n distinct lines, none of which are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, such that every point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1 lies on at least one of them.

This seems challenging. For n=3, we found such configuration: three lines each through a single point, but that covers only 3 points? Wait we had three points, and we used three lines each through one point, but then each line only contains that point. So union covers all three points. So that works. For n=4, points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 6 points. We have 4 lines. Can we cover all 6 points with 4 sunny lines? Possibly but need to cover each point. Could we use lines that cover many points? For instance, a line through (1,1) and (1,2) is vertical, not sunny. So can't. A line through (1,1) and (2,1) is horizontal, not sunny. A line through (1,2) and (2,1) has slope -1, not sunny. So any line that passes through two of the points is non-sunny. So if we want all lines sunny, we cannot have any line that contains two triangle points? But we could have a sunny line that contains a single triangle point, and then we need to cover all points with 4 lines, each covering at most one point? That would only cover at most 4 points, but we have 6 points. So we need each line to cover multiple points, but any line that covers multiple triangle points would have to be one of the non-sunny families? Not necessarily: Could a line with slope not 0,∞,-1 pass through two triangle points? For example, consider points (1,3) and (2,2). Their slope: (2-3)/(2-1) = -1, so slope -1. That's non-sunny. Points (1,2) and (3,1): slope (1-2)/(3-1)= -1/2, not -1,0,∞. That line is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0? Check: slope -1/2, not -1, so it is sunny. So it's possible to have a sunny line that contains two triangle points. So maybe we can cover many points with sunny lines.

Let's explore systematically.

We can think of the covering as a hypergraph or combinatorial design. The points are a triangular array. We need to assign each point to one or more lines, but lines can cover many points. The condition is just that the union covers all points.

So it seems that for n=5, the maximum number of points on a sunny line is 3, and only one line (y=x) achieves that. So we cannot cover 15 points with 5 lines each covering at most 3 points unless we have a partition into 5 sets of 3 collinear points. But we only have one such set. So maybe we need some lines to cover 4 points? Is it possible to have a sunny line with 4 points in the triangle for n=5? For slope 1, max 3. For slope 2, max 2. For slope 3, max 1. For slope 0, not sunny. For slope ∞, not sunny. For slope -1, not sunny. So no line with 4 points. So we need 5 disjoint collinear sets of 3 points each, each line sunny. But we only have one such set (y=x). So k=5 is impossible for n=5.

But wait, could we use lines that are not of the form y=mx+c? They could be vertical? Not sunny. So only non-vertical, non-horizontal, non-slope -1 lines. So we need to cover all 15 points with 5 sunny lines. That seems unlikely. However, we might be able to have some lines covering 2 points, and others covering 3, and still union covers all points if there is overlap? Overlap reduces the total distinct points covered, but we need 15 distinct points. If we have 5 lines, the total number of point incidences (counting multiplicity) is at most sum of points on each line. To cover 15 distinct points, the sum of points on each line must be at least 15, and if there is overlap, the sum could be >15, but that's okay. However, if some lines cover only 2 points, then the total sum of points on lines might be less than 15 if all lines cover 2 points? Actually if all 5 lines cover 2 points each, sum=10, but union could be at most 10, so cannot cover 15 points. So to cover 15 distinct points, the sum of points on lines must be at least 15. With maximum 3 per line, the sum of points on 5 lines is at most 15, so it must be exactly 15, and the lines must be pairwise disjoint (no overlap) in points. Because if any two lines share a point, then the total distinct points covered is less than the sum, so to reach 15 distinct points, the sum must be >15, but it cannot exceed 15 if each line ≤3. So indeed, for n=5, to cover all 15 points with 5 sunny lines, we would need each line to cover exactly 3 points, and the sets of points on each line must be disjoint. We have k=0 example with 5 vertical lines. k=1 with 4 vertical lines and one sunny line through (5,1). k=3 with 2 vertical lines (x=1,x=2) and 3 sunny lines (as we constructed). k=2 appears impossible, k=4 we haven't tried but likely impossible because 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny? But we need exactly k sunny lines, so k=4 would require 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny. That would be 1 non-sunny line covering many points, and 4 sunny lines covering the rest. With 4 sunny lines, maximum points they can cover is 4*3=12, but they might need to cover 15 - (points on non-sunny). If we use a non-sunny line covering, say, 5 points, then remaining 10 points need to be covered by 4 sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, 4 lines can cover at most 12, so possible. But can we actually find such a covering? For n=5, we have two sunny triples: y=x and y=-2x+7. That's 2 triples covering 6 points. To cover 10 points with 4 sunny lines, we would need 4 lines covering 10 distinct points, which means some lines cover 3, some 2, etc. But are there other sunny lines that contain 3 points? Only those two. So the only sunny lines that cover 3 points are those two. Other sunny lines cover at most 2 points. So with 4 sunny lines, the maximum distinct points they can cover is 3+3+2+2=10 if we use the two triples and two pairs. That's possible. So we could try to use the two sunny triples and two sunny pairs covering the remaining points. So we need to cover the 10 points not covered by the non-sunny line with 4 sunny lines: we could use y=x (covers 3 points: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3)), y=-2x+7 (covers 3 points: (1,5),(2,3),(3,1)), and then we need to cover the remaining 4 points (since total 10, we covered 6, need 4 more) with 2 sunny lines. Those 4 points are from the 15 points after removing non-sunny line. So we need to choose a non-sunny line such that the remaining 10 points contain these two triples and 4 other points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines (each covering 2 points). Is that possible? Let's try.

First, consider non-sunny line: maybe vertical x=1? That covers (1,1)-(1,5). Then remaining points: a=2,3,4,5 with b such that a+b≤6, excluding (1,1)-(1,5). That's 10 points: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4); (3,1),(3,2),(3,3); (4,1),(4,2); (5,1). Among these, the triple y=x: (2,2),(3,3) are present, but (1,1) is removed, so only 2 points. So not a triple. The triple y=-2x+7: (2,3) and (3,1) are present? (2,3) is in set, (3,1) in set, (1,5) removed. So only 2 points. So we cannot get a full triple from the remaining set. So if we use x=1 as non-sunny, then the sunny lines we could use would be limited to at most 2 points each. With 4 sunny lines, we could cover at most 4*2=8 distinct points, but we have 10 points to cover. So not enough. So we need a non-sunny line that leaves the two triples entirely within the remaining set. That means the non-sunny line should not intersect the triples. So we need a non-sunny line that avoids the points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) and (1,5),(2,3),(3,1). For n=5, the non-sunny line could be horizontal y=2? That covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2) - (2,2) is in triple y=x, so that would intersect. y=3 covers (1,3),(2,3),(3,3) - (2,3) in triple y=-2x+7, (3,3) in y=x, so intersects. y=4 covers (1,4),(2,4),(3,4) - (3,4) not in triangle? Actually (3,4) sum7>6 no; (1,4) not in triples, (2,4) not. So y=4 covers (1,4) and (2,4) only, but (2,4) is not in triples; (1,4) is not. So y=4 might avoid triples? Check: (1,4) is not in y=x (1,1) nor y=-2x+7? (1,4) gives 4 = -2+7=5? no. (2,4) gives 4 = -4+7=3? no. So y=4 does not intersect the triples. But it covers only 2 points (since (3,4) not in triangle). So that non-sunny line would cover 2 points. Then remaining points: 15-2=13 points. We need to cover them with 4 sunny lines. But sunny lines max 3 each, total max 12 distinct points, so impossible. So we need a non-sunny line that covers many points but avoids the triples. Could use a slope -1 line that avoids the triples. For example, y=-x+6 covers (2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). It includes (3,3) from y=x, so intersects. y=-x+5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) - includes (2,3) from y=-2x+7, so intersects. y=-x+4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) - includes (2,2) from y=x, (3,1) from y=-2x+7. So all slope -1 lines intersect the triples because the triples cover many sums. Actually the triples cover sums 2,4,6 for y=x and sums 4,5,6 for y=-2x+7? Wait, y=-2x+7 gives points with sums: (1,5):6, (2,3):5, (3,1):4. So it covers sums 4,5,6. y=x covers sums 2,4,6. So the union of these two triples covers sums 2,4,5,6. The slope -1 lines cover exactly one sum each. So any slope -1 line will intersect the triples if its sum is 2,4,5,6. For sum=2, that's (1,1) which is in y=x; sum=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) - (2,2) in y=x, (3,1) in y=-2x+7; sum=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2) - (2,3) in y=-2x+7; sum=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1) - (1,5) in y=-2x+7, (3,3) in y=x. So indeed all slope -1 lines with sum between 2 and 6 intersect the triples. So any non-sunny line that is slope -1 will intersect the triples. Similarly, vertical lines: x=1 covers (1,1) in y=x; x=2 covers (2,2) in y=x; x=3 covers (3,3) in y=x; x=4 covers (4,1) not in triples?
