Given the pattern, I think the correct answer is: for n=3, k=0,1,2,3; for n≥4, k=0,1,3. We need to provide a proof. I'll attempt to write a rigorous proof.

Proof outline:

Let T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a+b ≤ n+1}. Let L be a set of n distinct lines covering T_n. For a line L, define its type: H (horizontal), V (vertical), D (slope -1), or S (sunny, i.e., not H,V,D). Let s = number of sunny lines, t = n-s non-sunny lines.

We have sum_{L} |L ∩ T_n| ≥ |T_n| = N = n(n+1)/2.

Also, for a sunny line, |L ∩ T_n| ≤ M = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

We will prove that for n ≥ 4, s cannot be 2. 

Consider the diagonal D_{n+1} (points with a+b = n+1). It has n points. For any sunny line, it intersects D_{n+1} in at most 1 point. So the total number of points on D_{n+1} covered by sunny lines is at most s. Hence the number of points on D_{n+1} that must be covered by non-sunny lines is at least n - s. 

Now, consider the set of non-sunny lines. They are of three types: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. A horizontal line y=j intersects D_{n+1} in at most 1 point (specifically, (n+1-j, j) if j ≤ n). A vertical line x=i intersects D_{n+1} in at most 1 point (i, n+1-i). A slope -1 line y=-x+c intersects D_{n+1} in either 0 or all n points (if c = n+1). So a non-sunny line can cover all n points of D_{n+1} only if it is the line y=-x+(n+1). Otherwise, it covers at most 1 point. Therefore, if we want to cover the n - s points on D_{n+1} that are not covered by sunny lines, we have two options: either we use the slope -1 line for D_{n+1} (which covers all n points), or we use horizontal/vertical lines, each covering at most 1 point, so we need at least n - s such lines. Since we have only t = n - s non-sunny lines, the number of horizontal/vertical lines we can have is at most t. If we use the slope -1 line, then we have t-1 lines left for other diagonals. If we don't use it, then we need at least n - s horizontal/vertical lines to cover D_{n+1}, which forces t ≥ n - s, i.e., t ≥ n - s, which is true because t = n - s. So it's possible that all non-sunny lines are used as horizontal/vertical lines to cover D_{n+1}. 

Now, consider the diagonal D_{n} (points with a+b = n). It has n-1 points. The sunny lines cover at most s points on D_n. The remaining points on D_n must be covered by non-sunny lines. A non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point on D_n unless it is the slope -1 line for D_n (which would cover all n-1 points). So if we want to cover all points on D_n that are not covered by sunny lines, we need either to use the slope -1 line for D_n (which covers all points on D_n), or use enough horizontal/vertical lines to cover the points. Since we have limited horizontal/vertical lines, we need to see if it's possible.

We will use a counting argument on the total number of points on diagonals with small d_c. 

Let’s denote by p_c = c-1 the number of points on diagonal D_c. For c ≤ 3, p_c = 1 or 2. The points on D_2 and D_3 are 3 points. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 of these points (since on D_3, they can cover at most 1 point because covering both would require a slope -1 line). So at least 1 point on D_2∪D_3 must be covered by non-sunny lines. Similarly, on D_4, there are 3 points, and the sunny lines can cover at most 2 (since they cannot cover all 3). So at least 1 point on D_4 must be covered by non-sunny lines. For larger c, the sunny lines can cover at most 2 points, but the number of points on the diagonal is larger, so many points must be covered by non-sunny lines. 

We can sum the required coverage from non-sunny lines over all diagonals. For a diagonal D_c, let b_c be the number of points on D_c covered by sunny lines. Then b_c ≤ 2. The number of points that need to be covered by non-sunny lines is p_c - b_c ≥ p_c - 2 = c-3. So the total number of points that must be covered by non-sunny lines is at least ∑_{c=4}^{n+1} (c-3) = (n-2)(n-1)/2. Let’s denote R = (n-2)(n-1)/2. 

Now, consider the coverage provided by non-sunny lines. Let k be the number of diagonals that have a slope -1 line among the non-sunny lines. For each such diagonal c, the slope -1 line covers all p_c points. For the other diagonals, each point must be covered by vertical/horizontal lines. A vertical line x=i covers at most 1 point per diagonal, and the total number of points it can cover across all diagonals is at most n (since it covers points with x=i, which for c from i+1 to n+1? Actually x=i covers points (i,b) for b=1..n+1-i, so it covers points on diagonals c = i+1, i+2, ..., n+1? Because a+b=c => b=c-i, and b≥1 => c≥i+1; b≤n+1-i => c≤n+1. So x=i covers points on diagonals c = i+1,...,n+1. So it covers exactly (n+1-i) diagonals. So each vertical line covers at most n points (when i=1). Similarly, a horizontal line y=j covers points on diagonals c = j+1,...,n+1, covering at most n points. So the total number of points that vertical/horizontal lines can cover is at most (t-k) * n. 

Therefore, the total number of points that non-sunny lines can cover is at most S_k + (t-k)n, where S_k = sum of p_c over the k diagonals that have slope -1 lines. To maximize coverage, we would assign slope -1 lines to diagonals with the largest p_c. So the maximum possible coverage by non-sunny lines is at most ∑_{i=0}^{k-1} (n+1-i) + (t-k)n = k(2n+2-k)/2 + (n-2-k)n = n^2 -2n + k - k^2/2. This must be at least R. 

We already saw that for n≥4, this inequality holds for some k. But we also have the constraint from the small diagonals: the points on D_2 and D_3 must be covered. On D_2, there is 1 point; on D_3, 2 points. The sunny lines can cover at most 1 point on D_3 (since covering both would require slope -1). So at least 1 point on D_3 must be covered by non-sunny lines. Similarly, on D_2, if it is not covered by a sunny line, it must be covered by a non-sunny line. So overall, the number of points that must be covered by non-sunny lines on diagonals with c≤3 is at least 1. Therefore, the non-sunny lines must provide at least 1 point from these diagonals. That means either we have a slope -1 line that covers these diagonals, or we have vertical/horizontal lines that cover them. But slope -1 lines for D_2 or D_3 would be lines y=-x+2 and y=-x+3, which are not efficient because they cover few points. To minimize the number of vertical/horizontal lines needed, we would not use slope -1 lines for these small diagonals. So we would rely on vertical/horizontal lines. Thus we need at least 1 vertical/horizontal line. So t - k ≥ 1, i.e., k ≤ n-3.

Now, suppose we take k = n-3. Then we have n-3 slope -1 lines covering the largest n-3 diagonals (c = n+1, n, ..., (n-3+1)? Actually the largest n-3 diagonals are c = n+1, n, ..., n+1-(n-3)+1 = 4? Let's check: from c=n+1 down to c=4, that's n-2 diagonals? Actually the number of diagonals from 4 to n+1 is n-2. So n-3 lines can cover at most n-3 of them. We need to choose which ones. The optimal is to cover the largest ones, i.e., c=n+1, n, n-1, ..., (n-3+2?) The number of largest diagonals is n-3, so they would be c = n+1, n, ..., n+2-(n-3)=5? Actually the largest n-3 diagonals are c = n+1, n, ..., (n+1)-(n-3)+1 = 4? Wait, if we list from largest to smallest: n+1, n, n-1, ..., 4. That's n-2+? Let's compute: the number of diagonals from 4 to n+1 inclusive is (n+1)-4+1 = n-2. So there are n-2 such diagonals. We have n-3 lines, so we can cover at most n-3 of them. To cover the most points, we should cover the largest ones: c=n+1, n, ..., (n-2+2)? Actually the largest n-3 diagonals are c = n+1, n, n-1, ..., (n+1)-(n-3)+1 = 4? That would be n+1, n, n-1, ..., 4? That's n-2 terms. So n-3 lines cannot cover all n-2 diagonals; they cover n-3 of them, leaving 1 diagonal uncovered among those. So the uncovered diagonal among the larger ones is the smallest one, i.e., c=4. So we would have slope -1 lines for c=n+1, n, ..., 5, but not for c=4. Then we have 1 vertical/horizontal line to cover the points on diagonals c=4,3,2. But we also have the points on c=4 that need to be covered. Since we have a vertical/horizontal line, it can cover at most 1 point on c=4. The other points on c=4 must be covered by sunny lines. But sunny lines can cover at most 2 points on c=4 (since they intersect at most once each). So the total coverage on c=4 from sunny lines is at most 2, and we have 1 point from vertical/horizontal line, total 3 points, but c=4 has 3 points. So it's possible that sunny lines cover 2 points on c=4 and the vertical/horizontal line covers the remaining 1. However, we also need to cover points on c=3 and c=2. c=3 has 2 points, c=2 has 1 point. The sunny lines can cover at most 1 point on c=3 (since they cannot cover both), and possibly the point on c=2 if it lies on a sunny line. So the coverage from sunny lines on c=3 is at most 1, leaving at least 1 point on c=3 to be covered by non-sunny lines. But we only have 1 vertical/horizontal line. It can cover at most 1 point on c=3. So it's possible that the vertical/horizontal line covers that point. Similarly, c=2 point must be covered. Could be covered by a sunny line. So it's plausible that with k=n-3, we can cover all points. Let's test for n=5: k=2, use slope -1 lines for D_6 (5 points) and D_5 (4 points). That covers all points on those diagonals. Then we have 1 vertical/horizontal line. We need to cover points on D_4 (3 points), D_3 (2), D_2 (1). Total 6 points. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points on D_4, 1 on D_3, and 1 on D_2 (if it hits). So they can cover up to 2+1+1=4 points. The vertical/horizontal line can cover at most 1 point on each diagonal, so it can cover 1 point on D_4, 1 on D_3, 1 on D_2, but it can only cover one point total? Actually a vertical line x=i covers points on diagonals c = i+1,...,n+1. So it covers at most one point per diagonal, but it may cover points on multiple diagonals. In our case, the vertical/horizontal line can cover at most 1 point on each diagonal it hits, and it can hit up to n diagonals. So it could cover 1 point on D_4, 1 on D_3, 1 on D_2, covering all three of the small diagonals if it is placed appropriately. But then it would cover 3 points total, but we only have one line, which can cover at most n points (5 for n=5). So it's possible that the vertical/horizontal line covers the point on D_2, and also covers one point on D_3 and one on D_4. That would be 3 points. Then the sunny lines need to cover the remaining points: on D_3, there are 2 points, one covered by the line, one left; on D_4, there are 3 points, one covered by the line, two left; on D_2, none left. So we need to cover 1 point on D_3 and 2 points on D_4. That's 3 points. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points on D_4 and 1 on D_3. So they could cover 2 on D_4 and 1 on D_3, total 3 points, which is exactly the required. So this assignment seems feasible. We need to check if there exist two sunny lines L1 and L2 that together cover exactly 2 points on D_4 (the ones not covered by the line) and 1 point on D_3 (the one not covered by the line). And also the vertical/horizontal line must be such that it passes through the point on D_2, and one point on D_3, and one point on D_4. So we need to find a specific configuration.

For n=5, let's try to construct explicitly. We want to use slope -1 lines for D_6 and D_5. That's lines y=-x+6 and y=-x+5. Points: D_6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). D_5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So these are covered by non-sunny lines. Now, we need to cover the points on D_4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); D_3: (1,2),(2,1); D_2: (1,1). The two sunny lines must cover the points not covered by the vertical/horizontal line. The vertical/horizontal line can be chosen to cover one point on each of D_2,D_3,D_4. For instance, let's try a vertical line x=1. It passes through (1,1) on D_2, (1,2) on D_3, (1,3) on D_4. That's 3 points. Then the remaining points are: D_4: (2,2),(3,1); D_3: (2,1); D_2: none. So we need to cover (2,2),(3,1),(2,1) with 2 sunny lines. Can we find two sunny lines that cover these three points? Points: (2,2) and (3,1) are on a line of slope -1? (2,2)-(3,1) slope -1, not sunny. (2,2) and (2,1) vertical, not sunny. (3,1) and (2,1) horizontal, not sunny. So no single sunny line can contain two of them. Could we use a sunny line that covers (2,2) and (2,1)? That's vertical, not sunny. (2,2) and (3,1) slope -1 not sunny. (2,1) and (3,1) horizontal not sunny. So the only way to cover the three points with two sunny lines is to have each line cover one point, but then one point would be left uncovered because we have 2 lines and 3 points. So impossible.

Try a horizontal line y=1. It passes through (1,1) on D_2, (2,1) on D_3, (3,1) on D_4. Then remaining: D_4: (1,3),(2,2); D_3: (1,2); D_2: none. Need to cover (1,3),(2,2),(1,2) with 2 sunny lines. Try a vertical line x=2. It passes through (2,1) on D_3? Actually x=2: points (2,1) sum3, (2,2) sum4, (2,3) sum5, (2,4) sum6, (2,5) sum7. Need to cover (1,3),(3,1),(1,2),(1,1) with 2 sunny lines. That's 4 points. Can we cover them? Possibly one sunny line covers (1,1) and (3,1)? No, horizontal. (1,2) and (1,1) vertical. (1,3) and (1,2) vertical. So no. 

Try a vertical line x=3. It covers (3,1) on D_4, (3,2) on D_5, (3,3) on D_6. So it covers (3,1) on D_4. Then remaining: D_4: (1,3),(2,2); D_3: (1,2),(2,1); D_2: (1,1). That's 5 points. Too many.
